864 <section id="_1">

865

866 <h2>

867General Information About Testicular Cancer</h2>

868

869 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

870<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

871<h6>

872In This Section</h6>

922<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

923<a href="/types/testicular/patient/testicular-treatment-pdq">

924Go to Patient Version</a>

925</div>

926<section id="_385">

927<h3 id="_385_toc">

928Incidence and Mortality</h3>

929<p id="_450" tabindex="-1">

930Estimated new cases and deaths from testicular cancer in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9311</a>

932]</p>

933<div class="pdq-content-list">

934<ul id="_451">

935<li>

936New cases: 9,190.</li>

937<li>

938Deaths: 470.</li>

939</ul>

940</div>

941<p id="_452" tabindex="-1">

942Testicular cancer is a highly treatable, usually curable cancer that most often

943develops in young and middle-aged men. Most testicular cancers are germ cell tumors. For treatment planning, germ cell tumors are broadly divided

944into seminomas and nonseminomas because they have different prognostic and treatment algorithms. For patients with seminomas (all

945stages combined), the cure rate exceeds 90%. For patients with low-stage

946seminomas or nonseminomas, the cure rate approaches 100%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9472</a>

948-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9496</a>

950]</p>

951</section>

952<section id="_453">

953<h3 id="_453_toc">

954Risk Factors</h3>

955<p id="_454" tabindex="-1">

956Risk factors for testicular cancer include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

9577</a>

958]</p>

959<div class="pdq-content-list">

960<ul id="_455">

961<li>

962An undescended testis (cryptorchidism).</li>

963<li>

964A family history of testis cancer (particularly in a father or brother).</li>

965<li>

966A personal history of testis cancer.</li>

967</ul>

968</div>

969<p id="_456" tabindex="-1">

970Surgical correction of an undescended testis (orchiopexy) before puberty appears to lower the risk of testicular cancer, but this is not certain.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

9718</a>

972]</p>

973</section>

974<section id="_710">

975<h3 id="_710_toc">

976Histopathology</h3>

977<section id="_390">

978<h4 id="_390_toc">

979Types of testicular germ cell tumors: Seminomas versus nonseminomas</h4>

980<p id="_391" tabindex="-1">

981The five histopathological subtypes of testicular germ cell tumors include the following: </p>

982<div class="pdq-content-list">

983<ul id="_392">

984<li>

985Seminomas.</li>

986<li>

987Embryonal carcinomas.</li>

988<li>

989Teratomas.</li>

990<li>

991Yolk sac tumors.</li>

992<li>

993Choriocarcinomas.</li>

994</ul>

995</div>

996<p id="_393" tabindex="-1">

997Tumors that are 100% seminoma are considered seminomas. All other tumors, including those that have a mixture of seminoma and nonseminoma components, are considered and should be

998managed as nonseminomas. Most nonseminomas consist of a mixture of the different germ cell tumor subtypes. Tumors that appear to have a seminoma histology but are accompanied by an elevated

999serum level of alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) should be treated as nonseminomas because seminomas do not produce AFP.

1000</p>

1001</section>

1002</section>

1003<section id="_711">

1004<h3 id="_711_toc">

1005Prognosis and Staging</h3>

1006<section id="_562">

1007<h4 id="_562_toc">

1008Serum tumor markers and testis cancer: AFP, beta-hCG, and LDH</h4>

1009<p id="_563" tabindex="-1">

1010Alpha-fetoprotein (AFP), beta-human chorionic gonadotropin (beta-hCG), and lactase dehydrogenase (LDH) play an important role as serum tumor markers in the staging and monitoring of germ cell tumors and should be measured prior to removing the involved testicle.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

10119</a>

1012] For patients with nonseminomas, one of the most significant predictors of prognosis is the degree of tumor-marker elevation after the cancerous testicle has been removed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

101310</a>

1014] Elevated levels of serum tumor markers are often the earliest sign of relapse, making these markers useful for monitoring all stages of nonseminomas and metastatic seminomas. </p>

1015<p id="_564" tabindex="-1">

1016<strong>

1017AFP:</strong>

1018 Elevation of serum AFP is seen in 40% to 60% of men with nonseminomas. Seminomas do not produce AFP. Men who have an elevated serum AFP have a mixed germ cell tumor (i.e., nonseminomatous germ cell tumors [NSGCT]) even if the pathology shows a pure seminoma&#8212;unless there is a more persuasive explanation for the elevated AFP, such as liver disease. </p>

1019<p id="_565" tabindex="-1">

1020<strong>

1021Beta-hCG:</strong>

1022 Elevation of beta-hCG is

1023found in approximately 14% of patients with stage I pure seminomas before orchiectomy and in about half of patients with metastatic seminomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

102411</a>

1025-<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

102613</a>

1027] Approximately 40% to 60% of men with nonseminomas have an elevated serum beta-hCG. </p>

1028<p id="_566" tabindex="-1">

1029Significant and unambiguously rising levels of AFP and/or beta-hCG signal relapsed germ cell tumor in most cases and are an indication for treatment even in the absence of radiological evidence of metastatic disease. Nonetheless, tumor marker elevations need to be interpreted with caution. For example, false-positive beta-hCG levels can result from cross reactivity of the assay with luteinizing hormone, in which case an intramuscular injection of testosterone should result in normalization of beta-hCG values. There are also clinical reports of marijuana use resulting in elevations of serum beta-hCG and some experts recommend querying patients about drug use and retesting beta-hCG levels after a period of abstinence from marijuana use. Similarly, AFP is chronically mildly elevated in some individuals for unclear reasons and can be substantially elevated by liver disease. </p>

1030<p id="_567" tabindex="-1">

1031<strong>

1032LDH:</strong>

1033 Seminomas and nonseminomas alike may result in elevated LDH but such values are of unclear prognostic significance because LDH may be elevated in many conditions unrelated to cancer. A study evaluated the utility of LDH in 499 patients with a testicular germ cell tumor who were undergoing surveillance after orchiectomy or treatment of stage II or III disease. It found that 7.7% of patients had elevated LDH unrelated to cancer, while only 1.4% of patients had cancer-related increases in LDH.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

103414</a>

1035] Among 15 patients with relapsed disease, LDH was elevated in six patients and was the first sign of relapse in one patient. Over 9% of the men had a persistent false-positive increase in LDH. The positive predictive value for an elevated LDH was 12.8%. </p>

1036<p id="_568" tabindex="-1">

1037A second study reported that among 494 patients with stage I germ cell tumors who subsequently had a relapse, 125 had an elevated LDH at the time of relapse. Of these 125 patients, all had other evidence of relapse: 112 had a concurrent rise in AFP and/or beta-hCG, one had computed tomography (CT) evidence of relapse before the elevation in LDH, one had palpable disease on examination, and one complained of back pain that led to imaging that revealed retroperitoneal relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

103815</a>

1039] On one hand, measuring LDH appears to have little value for predicting relapse during surveillance of germ cell tumors. On the other hand, for patients with metastatic NSGCT, large studies of prognostic models have found the LDH level to be a significant independent predictor of survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

104010</a>

1041,<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

104216</a>

1043]</p>

1044</section>

1045<section id="_401">

1046<h4 id="_401_toc">

1047Staging and risk stratification</h4>

1048<p id="_402" tabindex="-1">

1049There are two major prognostic models for testicular cancer: staging[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

105017</a>

1051] and, for risk stratification of men with distant and/or bulky retroperitoneal metastases, the International Germ Cell Cancer Consensus Group classification.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

105210</a>

1053] The prognosis of patients with testicular germ cell tumors is determined by the following factors:</p>

1054<div class="pdq-content-list">

1055<ol id="_403">

1056<li>

1057Histology (seminoma vs. nonseminoma).</li>

1058<li>

1059The extent to which the tumor has spread (testis only vs. retroperitoneal lymph node involvement vs. pulmonary or distant nodal metastasis vs. nonpulmonary visceral metastasis).</li>

1060<li>

1061For nonseminomas, the degree to which serum tumor markers are elevated.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

106210</a>

1063]</li>

1064</ol>

1065</div>

1066<p id="_404" tabindex="-1">

1067For men with disseminated seminomas, the main adverse prognostic variable is the presence of metastases to organs other than the lungs (e.g., bone, liver, or brain). For men with disseminated nonseminomas, the following variables are independently associated with poor prognosis:</p>

1068<div class="pdq-content-list">

1069<ul id="_405">

1070<li>

1071Metastases to organs other than the lungs.</li>

1072<li>

1073Highly elevated serum tumor markers. </li>

1074<li>

1075Tumor that originated in the mediastinum rather than the testis.</li>

1076</ul>

1077</div>

1078<p id="_409" tabindex="-1">

1079Nonetheless, even patients with widespread metastases at

1080presentation, including those with brain metastases, may have curable disease and

1081should be treated with this intent.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

108218</a>

1083]</p>

1084<p id="_410" tabindex="-1">

1085Radical inguinal orchiectomy with initial high ligation of the spermatic cord

1086is the procedure of choice in diagnosing and treating a malignant testicular mass.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

108719</a>

1088]

1089As noted above, serum AFP, LDH, and beta-hCG should be measured before an orchiectomy. Transscrotal biopsy is not considered appropriate because of the risk of local

1090dissemination of tumor into the scrotum or its spread to inguinal lymph nodes. A

1091retrospective analysis of reported series in which transscrotal approaches were used showed a small but statistically significant increase in local

1092recurrence rates, compared with when the inguinal approach was used (2.9% vs. 0.4%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

109320</a>

1094][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1095Level of evidence C2</a>

1096] Distant recurrence and survival rates, however, were

1097indistinguishable in the two approaches. </p>

1098</section>

1099</section>

1100<section id="_712">

1101<h3 id="_712_toc">

1102Diagnostics</h3>

1103<p id="_412" tabindex="-1">

1104Evaluation of the retroperitoneal lymph nodes, usually by CT scanning, is an important aspect of

1105staging and treatment planning in adults with testicular cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

110621</a>

1107,<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

110822</a>

1109] Patients with a negative result have

1110a substantial chance of having microscopic involvement of the lymph nodes. Nearly 20% of patients with seminoma and 30% of patients with nonseminoma who have normal CT scans and serum tumor markers will subsequently relapse if not given additional treatment after orchiectomy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

111123</a>

1112-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

111325</a>

1114] For patients with nonseminoma, retroperitoneal lymph node dissection (RPLND) increases the accuracy of staging, but as many as 10% of men with normal imaging, normal tumor markers, and benign pathology at RPLND will still experience a relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

111526</a>

1116] After RPLND, about 25% of patients with clinical stage I

1117nonseminomatous testicular cancer are restaged as pathological stage II, and about 25% of clinical

1118stage II patients are restaged as pathological stage I.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

111926</a>

1120-<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

112128</a>

1122] In

1123prepubertal children, the use of serial measurements of AFP has proven sufficient for

1124monitoring response after initial orchiectomy. Lymphangiography and

1125para-aortic lymph node dissection do not appear to be useful or necessary in

1126the proper staging and management of testicular cancer in prepubertal boys.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

112729</a>

1128] For more information, see <a href="/types/testicular/hp/child-testicular-treatment-pdq">

1129Childhood Testicular Cancer Treatment</a>

1130.</p>

1131</section>

1132<section id="_713">

1133<h3 id="_713_toc">

1134Follow-up and Survivorship</h3>

1135<p id="_413" tabindex="-1">

1136Patients who have been cured of testicular cancer have approximately a 2% cumulative risk of developing cancer in the opposite testicle during the 15

1137years after initial diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

113830</a>

1139,<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

114031</a>

1141] Within this range, men with nonseminomatous primary tumors appear to have a lower risk of subsequent contralateral testis tumors than men with seminomas.</p>

1142<p id="_414" tabindex="-1">

1143Men with HIV are reported to be at increased risk of developing testicular

1144seminomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

114532</a>

1146] Depending on comorbid conditions such as active

1147infection, these men are generally managed similarly to patients without HIV.</p>

1148<p id="_415" tabindex="-1">

1149Because most patients with testicular cancer who receive adjuvant chemotherapy or radiation therapy are

1150curable, it is necessary to be aware of possible long-term effects of

1151 the various treatment modalities, such as the following:

1152</p>

1153<div class="pdq-content-list">

1154<ol id="_416">

1155<li>

1156<strong>

1157Fertility:</strong>

1158 Many patients have oligospermia or sperm abnormalities before

1159therapy, but semen analysis results generally become more normal after treatment. The impact of standard chemotherapy on fertility in patients with testicular cancer is not well defined, although it is well documented that most men can father children after treatment, often without the use of cryopreserved semen. In two large studies, roughly 70% of patients fathered children after treatment for testicular cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

116033</a>

1161,<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

116234</a>

1163] The likelihood of recovering fertility is related to the type of treatment received. The children do not appear

1164to have an increased risk of congenital malformations, but the data are not adequate to properly investigate this issue.[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

116535</a>

1166,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

116736</a>

1168] It is recommended that men wait at least 3 months after completing chemotherapy before conceiving a child (unless using cryopreserved sperm collected before chemotherapy was administered).[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

116936</a>

1170] <p id="_417" tabindex="-1">

1171Radiation therapy, used to treat pure seminomatous testicular cancers, can

1172cause fertility problems because of radiation scatter to the remaining testicle

1173during radiation therapy to retroperitoneal lymph nodes (as evidenced in the SWOG-8711 trial, for example).[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

117437</a>

1175] Depending on scatter

1176dose, sperm counts fall after radiation therapy but may recover over the course of 1

1177to 2 years. Shielding techniques can be used to decrease the radiation scatter

1178to the remaining normal testicle. Because chemotherapy, RPLND, and radiation therapy can each result in infertility, men can be offered the opportunity to bank sperm before undergoing any treatment for testicular cancer other than orchiectomy.</p>

1179</li>

1180<li>

1181<strong>

1182Secondary leukemias:</strong>

1183 Several reports of elevated risk of secondary acute

1184leukemia, primarily nonlymphocytic, have appeared.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

118538</a>

1186,<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

118739</a>

1188] An increased risk of leukemia has been associated with platinum-based chemotherapy and radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

118938</a>

1190] Etoposide-containing regimens are also associated with a

1191risk of secondary acute leukemias, usually in the myeloid lineage, and with a

1192characteristic 11q23 translocation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

119340</a>

1194,<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

119541</a>

1196] Etoposide-associated leukemias

1197typically occur sooner after therapy than alkylating agent-associated

1198leukemias and often show balanced chromosomal translocations on the long arm of

1199chromosome 11. Standard etoposide dosages (&lt;2 g/m<span class="sup">

12002</span>

1201 cumulative dose) are associated with a relative risk of 15 to 25, but this

1202translates into a cumulative incidence of leukemia of less than 0.5% at 5

1203years. Preliminary data suggest that cumulative doses of more than 2 g/m<span class="sup">

12042</span>

1205 of etoposide may confer higher risk.

1206</li>

1207<li>

1208<strong>

1209Renal function:</strong>

1210 Minor decreases in creatinine clearance occur (about a 15%

1211decrease, on average) during platinum-based therapy, but they appear to remain

1212stable in the long term, without significant deterioration.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

121342</a>

1214]</li>

1215<li>

1216<strong>

1217Hearing:</strong>

1218 Bilateral hearing deficits occur with cisplatin-based

1219chemotherapy, but they generally occur at sound frequencies of 4 kHz to 8

1220kHz, which is outside the range of conversational tones. Therefore, hearing

1221aids are rarely required if standard doses of cisplatin are administered.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

122242</a>

1223]</li>

1224<li>

1225<strong>

1226Lung function:</strong>

1227 A study of pulmonary function tests in 1,049 long-term survivors of testicular cancer reported a cisplatin-dose-dependent increase in the incidence of restrictive lung disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

122843</a>

1229] Whereas men receiving up to 850 mg of cisplatin had a normal risk of restrictive lung disease, men who received over 850 mg of cisplatin had a threefold increased risk. In absolute terms, patients who received no chemotherapy had an incidence of restrictive lung disease of less than 8%, whereas the incidence of restrictive lung disease among those receiving over 850 mg of cisplatin was nearly 18%. However, only 9.5% of those with pulmonary function testing indicative of restrictive lung disease reported dyspnea. Although cisplatin was more strongly associated with decreased lung function in this study, cumulative bleomycin dose was also associated with a decline in forced vital capacity and the 1-second forced expiratory volume (FEV1) but not with restrictive lung disease.</li>

1230</ol>

1231</div>

1232<p id="_418" tabindex="-1">

1233Although acute pulmonary toxic effects may occur with bleomycin, they are rarely fatal at

1234total cumulative doses of less than 400 units. Because life-threatening

1235pulmonary toxic effects can occur, the drug should be discontinued if early

1236signs of pulmonary toxic effects develop. Although decreases in pulmonary

1237function are frequent, they are rarely symptomatic and are reversible after chemotherapy ends. Survivors of testis cancer who were treated with chemotherapy have been reported to be at increased risk of death from respiratory diseases, but it is unknown whether this finding is related to bleomycin exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

123844</a>

1239]</p>

1240<p id="_419" tabindex="-1">

1241Radiation therapy, often used in the management of pure seminomatous germ cell

1242cancers, has been linked to the development of secondary cancers, especially

1243solid tumors in the radiation portal, usually after a latency period of a

1244decade or more.[<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

124545</a>

1246,<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

124746</a>

1248] These secondary cancers include melanoma and cancers of the stomach, bladder, colon,

1249rectum, pancreas, lung, pleura, prostate, kidney, connective tissue, and thyroid.

1250Chemotherapy has also been associated with an elevated risk of secondary cancers.</p>

1251<section id="_715">

1252<h4 id="_715_toc">

1253Other risk factors</h4>

1254<section id="_420">

1255<h5 id="_420_toc">

1256Cardiovascular disease in testicular cancer survivors</h5>

1257<p id="_421" tabindex="-1">

1258Men with testicular cancer who have been treated with radiation therapy and/or chemotherapy have been reported to be at increased risk of cardiovascular events.[<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

125947</a>

1260-<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

126149</a>

1262] Other studies have reported that chemotherapy for testicular cancer is associated with an increased risk of developing metabolic syndrome and hypogonadism.[<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

126350</a>

1264,<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

126551</a>

1266] Moreover, an international population-based study reported that men treated with either radiation therapy or chemotherapy were at increased risk of death from circulatory diseases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

126744</a>

1268]</p>

1269<p id="_459" tabindex="-1">

1270 In a retrospective series of 992 patients treated for testicular cancer between 1982 and 1992, cardiac events were increased approximately 2.5-fold in patients treated with radiation therapy and/or chemotherapy, compared with those who underwent surveillance for a median of 10.2 years. The actuarial risks of cardiac events were 7.2% for patients who received radiation therapy (92% of whom did not receive mediastinal radiation therapy), 3.4% for patients who received chemotherapy (primarily platinum-based), 4.1% for patients who received combined therapy, and 1.4% for patients who underwent surveillance management after 10 years of follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

127148</a>

1272]</p>

1273<p id="_423" tabindex="-1">

1274 A population-based retrospective study of 2,339 testicular cancer survivors in the Netherlands, treated between 1965 and 1995 and followed for a median of 18.4 years, found that the overall incidence of coronary heart disease (i.e., myocardial infarction and/or angina pectoris) was increased 1.17 times (95% confidence interval [CI], 1.04&#8211;1.31) compared with the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

127549</a>

1276] Patients who received radiation therapy to the mediastinum had a 2.5-fold (95% CI, 1.8&#8211;3.4) increased risk of coronary heart disease, and those who also received chemotherapy had an almost threefold (95% CI, 1.7&#8211;4.8) increased risk. Patients who were treated with infradiaphragmatic radiation therapy alone had no significantly increased risk of coronary heart disease. In multivariate Cox regression analyses, the older chemotherapy regimen of cisplatin, vinblastine, and bleomycin, used until the mid-1980s, was associated with a significant 1.9-fold (95% CI, 1.2&#8211;2.9) increased risk of cardiovascular disease (i.e., myocardial infarction, angina pectoris, and heart failure combined). The newer regimen of bleomycin, etoposide, and cisplatin was associated with a borderline significant 1.5-fold (95% CI, 1.0&#8211;2.2) increased risk of cardiovascular disease. Similarly, an international pooled analysis of population-based databases reported that the risk of death from circulatory disease was increased in men treated with chemotherapy (standardized mortality ratio [SMR] = 1.58) or radiation therapy (SMR = 1.70).[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

127744</a>

1278][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1279Level of evidence C2</a>

1280]</p>

1281<p id="_424" tabindex="-1">

1282Although testicular cancer is highly curable, all newly diagnosed patients are

1283appropriate candidates for clinical trials designed to decrease

1284morbidity of treatment while further improving cure rates.

1285</p>

1286</section>

1501 <section id="_21">

1502

1503 <h2>

1504Cellular Classification of Testicular Cancer</h2>

1505

1506 <div id="_section_21" class="pdq-sections">

1507<p id="_248" tabindex="-1">

1508The following histological classification of malignant testicular germ cell tumors (testicular cancer) reflects the classification used by the World Health Organization (WHO).[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

15091</a>

1510] Less than 50% of malignant testicular germ cell tumors have a single cell type, approximately 50% of which are seminomas. The remaining tumors have more than one cell type, and the relative proportions of each cell type should be specified. The cell type of these tumors is important for estimating the risk of metastases and the response to chemotherapy. Polyembryoma presents an unusual growth pattern and is sometimes listed as a single histological type, though it might better be regarded as a mixed tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

15111</a>

1512-<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

15133</a>

1514]</p>

1515<div class="pdq-content-list">

1516<ol id="_249" class="upper-alpha">

1517<li>

1518 Intratubular germ cell neoplasia, unclassified.</li>

1519<li>

1520 Malignant pure germ cell tumor (showing a single-cell type):<div class="pdq-content-list">

1521<ol id="_250">

1522<li>

1523Seminoma.</li>

1524<li>

1525 Embryonal carcinoma.</li>

1526<li>

1527 Teratoma.</li>

1528<li>

1529 Choriocarcinoma.</li>

1530<li>

1531 Yolk sac tumor.</li>

1532</ol>

1533</div>

1534</li>

1535<li>

1536Malignant mixed germ cell tumor (showing more than one histological pattern):<div class="pdq-content-list">

1537<ol id="_251">

1538<li>

1539 Embryonal carcinoma and teratoma with or without seminoma. </li>

1540<li>

1541 Embryonal carcinoma and yolk sac tumor with or without seminoma. </li>

1542<li>

1543 Embryonal carcinoma and seminoma. </li>

1544<li>

1545 Yolk sac tumor and teratoma with or without seminoma.</li>

1546<li>

1547 Choriocarcinoma and any other element.</li>

1548</ol>

1549</div>

1550</li>

1551<li>

1552 Polyembryoma.</li>

1553</ol>

1554</div>

1555<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1556References</h6>

1557<ol>

1560<li id="section_2.2">

1561Ulbright TM, Berney DM: Testicular and paratesticular tumors. In: Mills SE, Carter D, Greenson JK, et al., eds.: Sternberg's Diagnostic Surgical Pathology. Lippincott Williams &amp; Wilkins, 2010, pp 1944-2004.</li>

1562<li id="section_2.3">

1564</ol>

1592classification to define testicular cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

15931</a>

1594]</p>

2571</section>

2572<section id="_51">

2573<p id="_244" tabindex="-1">

2574In addition to the clinical stage definitions, surgical stage may be designated

2575based on the results of surgical removal and microscopic examination of tissue.</p>

2576<section id="_727">

2577<p id="_728" tabindex="-1">

2578<strong>

2579Stage 0</strong>

2580</p>

2581<p id="_719" tabindex="-1">

2582Stage 0 testicular cancer is testicular intraepithelial neoplasia (TIN), also referred to as intratubular germ cell neoplasia (ITGCN). TIN is analogous to carcinoma <em>

2583in situ</em>

2584. In most cases, TIN is diagnosed as a result of an orchiectomy that was performed to remove an invasive germ cell tumor (pT1&#8211;T4); generally, TIN has already been removed from the body at the time of diagnosis and requires no treatment. A more challenging situation arises if a biopsy is performed of the contralateral testis and TIN is discovered. Because of the low incidence and low mortality rates associated with contralateral germ cell tumors, such biopsies are performed rarely in the United States. Therefore, TIN is almost never diagnosed in testicles that do not also have an invasive tumor. Consequently, a treatment decision about TIN in stage 0 testicular cancer is rarely faced in the United States. Treatment options for ITGCN include radiation therapy, surveillance, and orchiectomy.

2585</p>

2586</section>

2587<section id="_79">

2588<p id="_232" tabindex="-1">

2589<strong>

2590Stage I</strong>

2591</p>

2592<p id="_80" tabindex="-1">

2593Stage I testicular cancer is limited to the testis. Invasion of the scrotal

2594wall by tumor or interruption of the scrotal wall by previous surgery does not

2595change the stage but does increase the risk of spread to the inguinal lymph

2596nodes, and this must be considered in treatment and follow-up. Invasion of the

2597epididymis tunica albuginea and/or the rete testis does not change the

2598stage. Invasion of the tunica vaginalis or lymphovascular invasion signifies a T2 tumor, while invasion of the spermatic cord signifies a T3 tumor, and invasion of the scrotum signifies a T4. Increases in T stage are associated with increased risk of occult metastatic disease and recurrence. Men with stage I disease who have persistently elevated serum tumor markers after orchiectomy are staged as IS, but stage IS nonseminomas are treated as stage III. Elevated serum tumor markers in stage I or II seminoma are of unclear significance except that a persistently elevated or rising beta-hCG usually indicates metastatic disease.</p>

2599</section>

2600<section id="_81">

2601<p id="_233" tabindex="-1">

2602<strong>

2603Stage II</strong>

2604</p>

2605<p id="_82" tabindex="-1">

2606Stage II testicular cancer involves the testis and the retroperitoneal or

2607periaortic lymph nodes usually in the region of the kidney. Retroperitoneal

2608involvement should be further characterized by the number of nodes involved and

2609the size of involved nodes. The risk of recurrence is increased if more than five

2610nodes are involved or if the size of one or more involved nodes is more than 2

2611cm. Bulky stage II disease

2612(stage IIC) describes patients with extensive retroperitoneal nodes (&gt;5 cm), which portends a less favorable prognosis.

2613</p>

2614</section>

2615<section id="_269">

2616<p id="_234" tabindex="-1">

2617<strong>

2618Stage III</strong>

2619</p>

2620<p id="_84" tabindex="-1">

2621Stage III disease has spread beyond the retroperitoneal nodes based on physical

2622examination, imaging studies, and/or blood tests (i.e., patients with retroperitoneal adenopathy and highly elevated serum tumor markers are stage III). Stage III can be further stratified based on the location of metastasis and the degree of elevation of serum tumor markers. In the favorable group (IIIA), metastases are

2623limited to lymph nodes and lung, and serum tumor markers are no more than mildly elevated. Stage IIIB patients have moderately elevated tumor markers, while stage IIIC patients have highly elevated markers and/or metastases to liver, bone, brain, or some organ other than the lungs. These subclassifications of stage III correspond to the International Germ Cell Consensus Classification system for disseminated germ cell tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

26242</a>

2625]</p>

2626</section>

2627</section>

2628<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2629References</h6>

2630<ol>

2632Brimo F, Srigley J, Ryan C: Testis. In: Amin MB, Edge SB, Greene FL, et al., eds.: AJCC Cancer Staging Manual. 8th ed. Springer; 2017, pp. 727&#8211;35.</li>

2633<li id="section_3.2">

2634International Germ Cell Consensus Classification: a prognostic factor-based staging system for metastatic germ cell cancers. International Germ Cell Cancer Collaborative Group. J Clin Oncol 15 (2): 594-603, 1997.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=9053482&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=9053482&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2635[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2636</li>

2637</ol>

2638</div>

2643

2644 <section id="_85">

2645

2646 <h2>

2647Treatment Option Overview</h2>

2648

2649 <div id="_section_85" class="pdq-sections">

2650<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2651<h6>

2652In This Section</h6>

2653<ul>

2654<li>

2655<a href="#_88_toc">

2656Good Prognosis</a>

2657</li>

2658<li>

2659<a href="#_98_toc">

2660Intermediate Prognosis</a>

2661</li>

2662<li>

2663<a href="#_108_toc">

2664Poor Prognosis</a>

2665</li>

2666</ul>

2667</nav>

2668<p id="_86" tabindex="-1">

2669Testicular cancer is broadly divided into seminomas and nonseminomas for

2670treatment planning. Seminomatous types of testicular cancer are more

2671sensitive to radiation therapy and chemotherapy and are less prone to distant metastases than nonseminomatous types. Nonseminomas may include teratomatous elements, which tend to be resistant to chemotherapy and often require surgery for cure. By definition, pure seminomas do not contain elements of teratoma. Therefore, surgery plays a larger role in the management of nonseminomas than in the management of seminomas. Nonseminomatous testicular tumors include the following:

2672</p>

2673<div class="pdq-content-list">

2674<ul id="_317">

2675<li>

2676Embryonal carcinomas.</li>

2677<li>

2678Yolk

2679sac tumors.</li>

2680<li>

2681Choriocarcinomas. </li>

2682<li>

2683Teratomas.</li>

2684<li>

2685Mixed germ cell tumors.</li>

2686</ul>

2687</div>

2688<p id="_87" tabindex="-1">

2689An international germ cell tumor prognostic classification has been developed

2690based on a retrospective analysis of 5,202 patients with metastatic

2691nonseminomatous and 660 patients with metastatic seminomatous germ cell

2692tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

26931</a>

2694] All patients received treatment with cisplatin- or carboplatin-containing therapy as their first chemotherapy course. The prognostic

2695classification, shown below, was agreed on in 1997 by all major clinical

2696trial groups worldwide. It is used for reporting clinical trial

2697results of patients with germ cell tumors.

2698</p>

2699<p id="_278" tabindex="-1">

2700A meta-analysis of treatment outcomes for patients with advanced nonseminoma suggested that 5-year survival rates have improved for those patients with a poor prognosis during the period of 1989 to 2004.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

27012</a>

2702] In addition to improved therapy, the improvement in survival rates could be the result of publication bias, changes in patient selection in reported clinical trials, or more sensitive staging methods that could migrate less-advanced stages to more-advanced stage categories (i.e., stage migration).</p>

2703<section id="_88">

2709</p>

2726</li>

2729</li>

2897 <section id="_721">

2898

2899 <h2>

2900Treatment of Stage 0 Testicular Cancer</h2>

2901

2902 <div id="_section_721" class="pdq-sections">

2903<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2904<h6>

2905In This Section</h6>

2906<ul>

2907<li>

2908<a href="#_TrialSearch_721_sid_5_toc">

2909Current Clinical Trials</a>

2910</li>

2911</ul>

2912</nav>

2913<p id="_722" tabindex="-1">

2914Among men diagnosed with an invasive testicular germ cell tumor (stages I&#8211;III), 0.5% to 1.0% will present with tumors in both testes, and another 1% to 2% will develop a subsequent invasive germ cell tumor in the contralateral testis.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

29151</a>

2916-<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

29173</a>

2918] Death from metachronous contralateral germ cell tumors is rare. One study of 29,515 U.S. men with testicular germ cell tumors who were diagnosed between 1973 and 2001 reported that 287 men developed a metachronous contralateral testis cancer, one of whom died.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

29193</a>

2920] As a result, there is limited rationale for performing biopsies to search for testicular intraepithelial neoplasia (TIN) in men diagnosed with invasive testicular cancer. </p>

2921<p id="_723" tabindex="-1">

2922If biopsies of the contralateral testis are performed in men with testicular cancer, 4% to 8% of men will be found to have contralateral TIN. The treatment is typically radiation therapy (18 Gy&#8211;20 Gy), surveillance, or orchiectomy. Men undergoing radiation therapy or orchiectomy will subsequently be sterile. Men undergoing orchiectomy will also be hypogonadal as will many men undergoing radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

29234</a>

2924]</p>

2925<p id="_724" tabindex="-1">

2926<strong>

2927Treatment options:</strong>

2928</p>

2929<div class="pdq-content-list">

2930<ol id="_725">

2931<li>

2932Radiation therapy for TIN is associated with a low risk of relapse. One study of 122 patients with TIN treated with 18 Gy to 20 Gy of external-beam radiation therapy reported three relapses (2.5%).[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

29334</a>

2934]</li>

2935<li>

2936Surveillance with annual transscrotal ultrasonography and monthly self-examinations are also options for men with TIN. Approximately one-half of the TIN cases will progress to invasive germ cell tumors with a median time to progression of roughly 3 years.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

29374</a>

2938]</li>

2939<li>

2940Chemotherapy does not appear to be very effective at preventing the development of invasive testicular germ cell tumors. One series reported progression to invasive cancers in 10 of 30 patients treated with two cycles of bleomycin, etoposide and cisplatin (BEP); the same progression was found in 7 of 51 patients treated with three or more cycles of BEP; 2 of 15 patients treated with carboplatin also showed a progression to invasive cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

29414</a>

2942,<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

29435</a>

2944]</li>

2945</ol>

2946</div>

2948<h3 id="_TrialSearch_721_sid_5_toc">

2949Current Clinical Trials</h3>

2950<p id="_TrialSearch_721_22" tabindex="-1">

2951Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

2952advanced clinical trial search</a>

2953 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

2954General information</a>

2955 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

2988

2989 <h2>

2990Treatment of Stage I Testicular Cancer</h2>

2991

2992 <div id="_section_117" class="pdq-sections">

2993<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2994<h6>

2995In This Section</h6>

2996<ul>

2997<li>

2998<a href="#_120_toc">

2999Stage I Seminoma</a>

3000</li>

3001<li>

3002<a href="#_125_toc">

3003Stage I Nonseminoma</a>

3004</li>

3005<li>

3006<a href="#_TrialSearch_117_sid_6_toc">

3011<section id="_120">

3012<h3 id="_120_toc">

3013Stage I Seminoma</h3>

3014<p id="_121" tabindex="-1">

3015Patients with stage I seminomas have a cure rate that approaches 100%, regardless of whether postorchiectomy adjuvant therapy is given.

3016</p>

3017<p id="_425" tabindex="-1">

3018<strong>

3019Standard treatment options:</strong>

3020</p>

3021<div class="pdq-content-list">

3022<ul id="_694">

3023<li>

3024Radical inguinal orchiectomy with no retroperitoneal node radiation therapy followed

3025by chest x-rays and computed tomography (CT) scans

3026of the abdomen and pelvis (surveillance). These studies are typically performed every 4 months for the first 3 years, then every 6 months for 3 years, and then annually for an additional 4 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

30271</a>

3028]<p id="_695" tabindex="-1">

3029Results of multiple clinical series, including more than 1,200

3030patients with stage I seminoma managed by postorchiectomy surveillance, have

3031been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

30322</a>

3033-<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

30349</a>

3035] The overall 10-year tumor recurrence rate is 15% to 20%, and nearly

3036all patients whose disease recurred were cured by radiation therapy or

3037chemotherapy. Thus, the overall cure rate is indistinguishable from that

3038achieved with adjuvant radiation therapy or carboplatin chemotherapy. Relapses after 5 years are unusual but can occur in as many as 4% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

30396</a>

3040] Independent risk factors for relapse include tumor size greater than 4 cm and invasion of the rete testis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

30412</a>

3042] The 5-year risk of relapse is about 10% without either risk factor, 16% with one risk factor, and 32% with both risk factors. </p>

3043 </li>

3044</ul>

3045</div>

3046<p id="_696" tabindex="-1">

3047<strong>

3048Treatment options when surveillance is not chosen:</strong>

3049</p>

3050<p id="_697" tabindex="-1">

3051The surveillance-after-orchiectomy treatment option is associated with a cure rate that approaches 100%. Relapses requiring additional therapy occur in about 15% of patients who are treated with the surveillance treatment option. The surveillance strategy avoids the need for radiation or chemotherapy in most patients. However, some patients are uncomfortable with surveillance only and wish to minimize the risk of relapse. For such patients, one of the following options may be used; however, there is controversy about which strategy is preferred:[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

305210</a>

3053]</p>

3054<div class="pdq-content-list">

3055<ol id="_258">

3056<li>

3057Removal of the testicle via radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by radiation

3058therapy is an approach that is associated with a 5-year relapse-free survival (RFS) rate of 95% to 96% and a 5-year disease-specific survival rate over 99% in multiple large series and randomized controlled trials.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

305911</a>

3060-<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

306117</a>

3062]<p id="_698" tabindex="-1">

3063One of the following two treatment fields is typically used: a para-aortic strip covering the retroperitoneal nodes or a dog-leg field that includes the ipsilateral iliac lymph nodes as well as the retroperitoneum. The dose ranges from 20 Gy to 26 Gy. Relapse rates and toxic effects were studied in a randomized

3064comparison (MRC-TE10) of para-aortic radiation therapy alone versus para-aortic radiation

3065therapy with an added ipsilateral iliac lymph node field.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

306613</a>

3067,<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

306818</a>

3069] The 5-year

3070RFS rates were virtually identical (96.1% for patients who were treated with the para-aortic strip vs. 96.2% for patients who were treated by a dog-leg field) as

3071were overall survival (OS) rates (one death from seminoma occurred in the para-aortic radiation therapy arm). Pelvic RFS

3072rates were 98.2% versus 100%; the 95% confidence interval (CI) for the difference in

3073pelvic RFS rates was 0% to 3.7%. A statistically

3074significant increase was observed in leukopenia and diarrhea associated with the ipsilateral

3075iliac radiation therapy.</p>

3076<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

3077 In a randomized trial (MRC-TE18), a radiation dose of 20 Gy over 10 daily fractions was clinically equivalent to 30 Gy over 15 fractions after a median follow-up of 7 years in both RFS and OS. Patients reported that lethargy and their ability to perform normal work were better in the lower-dose regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

307814</a>

3079,<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

308018</a>

3081][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3082Level of evidence A1</a>

3083]</p>

3084<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

3085Radiation therapy for clinical stage I testicular seminoma is no longer favored because of evidence that this treatment is associated with an increased risk of secondary malignancies and an increased risk of death from secondary malignancies. An analysis of data from the population-based Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program registries in the United States between the years 1973 and 2001 indicated that among 7,179 men receiving radiation therapy for stage I seminoma, 246 had an increased risk of death from secondary cancers compared with the general population (standardized mortality ratio, 1.89; 95% CI, 1.67&#8211;2.14).[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

308619</a>

3087] An international study of more than 40,000 testicular cancer survivors reported that among the 7,885 survivors who had been followed for 20 to 29 years, radiation therapy was associated with a doubling of the risk of secondary cancers (relative risk, 2.0; 95% CI, 1.8&#8211;2.3).[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

308820</a>

3089]</p>

3090</li>

3091<li>

3092Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by either one or two doses of carboplatin adjuvant therapy. <p id="_429" tabindex="-1">

3093In a large, randomized, controlled, noninferiority trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003014">

3094MRC-TE19</a>

3095 [NCT00003014]), 1,477 men with stage I seminomas were assigned to undergo para-aortic (or dog-leg field, if clinically indicated) radiation therapy or to receive a single dose of carboplatin (concentration-versus-time or area-under-the-curve [AUC] &#215; 7) after radical inguinal orchiectomy study participants were followed up for a median of 6.5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

309618</a>

3097,<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

309821</a>

3099] The RFS rate at 5 years was 94.7% in the carboplatin arm and 96.0% in the radiation therapy arm (1.3% difference; 90% CI, 0.7%&#8211;3.5%; hazard ratio [HR], 1.25 [nonsignificant trend in favor of radiation therapy]; 90% CI, 0.83&#8211;1.89). The one death from seminoma occurred in the radiation therapy arm. There was a reduced number of contralateral testicular germ cell tumors in the carboplatin arm: 2 versus 15 (HR, 0.22; 95% CI, 0.05&#8211;0.95; <em>

3100P</em>

3101 = .03).[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

310221</a>

3103][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3104Level of evidence A1</a>

3105] In this trial, AUC dosing was based on radioisotope measurement of glomerular filtration rate; dosing based on calculations of creatinine clearance is not equivalent, has not been validated in this setting, and is discouraged. </p>

3106<p id="_430" tabindex="-1">

3107Phase II studies, including several with more than 4 years median follow-up, have consistently reported lower relapse rates (0%&#8211;3.3%) when two doses of carboplatin were administered either 3 or 4 weeks apart and dosed either at 400 mg/m<span class="sup">

31082</span>

3109 or at an AUC of 7.[<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

31103</a>

3111,<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

31124</a>

3113,<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

311422</a>

3115-<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

311626</a>

3117] Administration of two doses of carboplatin has never been compared with a single dose nor with radiation therapy in a randomized trial.</p>

3118</li>

3119</ol>

3120</div>

3121</section>

3122<section id="_125">

3123<h3 id="_125_toc">

3124Stage I Nonseminoma</h3>

3125<p id="_126" tabindex="-1">

3126Stage I nonseminoma is highly curable (&gt;99%). Orchiectomy alone will cure about 70% of patients, but the remaining 30% will relapse and require additional treatment. The relapses are highly curable, and postorchiectomy surveillance is a standard treatment option, but some physicians and patients prefer to reduce the risk of relapse by having the patient undergo either a retroperitoneal lymph node dissection (RPLND) or one or two cycles of chemotherapy. Each of these three approaches has unique advantages and disadvantages, and none has been shown to result in longer survival or superior quality of life.</p>

3127<p id="_127" tabindex="-1">

3128<strong>

3129Standard treatment options:</strong>

3130

3131</p>

3132<div class="pdq-content-list">

3133<ol id="_660">

3134<li>

3135Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by a regular and frequent surveillance schedule.<p id="_661" tabindex="-1">

3136Typically, patients are seen monthly during the first year, every 2 months during the second year, every 3 months during the third year, every 4 months during the fourth year, every 6 months during the fifth year, and annually for the subsequent 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

313727</a>

3138-<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

313929</a>

3140] At each visit, the history is reviewed, a physical examination is given, determination of serum markers are performed, and a chest x-ray is obtained (sometimes at alternating visits). An additional key aspect of surveillance involves abdominal or abdominopelvic CT scans, but the preferred frequency of such scans is controversial.</p>

3141<p id="_662" tabindex="-1">

3142A randomized controlled trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003420">

3143MRC-TE08</a>

3144 [NCT00003420]) compared a schedule that used only two scans at 3 months and 12 months with a schedule that used five scans at 3, 6, 9, 12, and 24 months.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

314530</a>

3146] With over 400 randomly assigned patients and a median follow-up of 40 months, all relapsing patients had either good- or intermediate-risk disease, and there were no differences in the stage or extent of disease at relapse between the two arms. No deaths were reported. Nonetheless, some organizations recommend CT scans every 3 to 4 months during the first 3 years of follow-up and continuing but less-frequent CT scans thereafter. While this study would appear to indicate that scans at 3 and 12 months are adequate during the first year, longer follow-up will be needed to assess whether discontinuing scans after 12 months is safe.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

314730</a>

3148][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3149Level of evidence A1</a>

3150] With regard to chest imaging, disease recurrence is rarely detected by chest x-ray alone, so chest x-ray may play little or no role in routine surveillance but is nonetheless included in the mainstream surveillance schedules.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

315127</a>

3152]</p>

3153<p id="_663" tabindex="-1">

3154The need for long-term follow-up has not been adequately investigated. Surveillance series with long follow-up times have reported that fewer than 1% of clinical stage I patients relapse after 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

315531</a>

3156,<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

315732</a>

3158] Late relapses often occur in the retroperitoneum when they do occur. Therefore, some schedules discontinue CT scans after 12 months, while others recommend at least annual scans for 10 years.</p>

3159<p id="_664" tabindex="-1">

3160The option of a radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by a regular and frequent surveillance schedule should be considered only if:</p>

3161<div class="pdq-content-list">

3162<ul id="_709">

3163<li>

3164CT scan and serum markers are negative.</li>

3165<li>

3166The patient accepts the need for and commits to frequent surveillance visits. Children are adequately followed by alpha-fetoprotein serum markers, chest x-rays, and clinical examination.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

316733</a>

3168]</li>

3169<li>

3170The physician accepts responsibility for seeing that a follow-up schedule is maintained as noted. </li>

3171</ul>

3172</div>

3173</li>

3174<li>

3175Removal of the testicle through the groin followed (in adults) by

3176RPLND.<p id="_666" tabindex="-1">

3177A nerve-sparing RPLND that preserves ejaculation in virtually every patient has

3178been described in clinical stage I patients and appears to be as effective as

3179the standard RPLND.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

318034</a>

3181-<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

318236</a>

3183] Surgery should be followed by monthly

3184determination of serum markers and chest x-rays for the first year and every-other-month determinations for the second year.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

318527</a>

3186]</p>

3187<p id="_667" tabindex="-1">

3188Men undergoing RPLND, who are found to have pathological stage I disease, have a roughly 10% risk of relapsing subsequently, whereas men with pathological stage II disease (i.e., those who are found to have lymph node metastases at RPLND) have as much as a 50% risk of relapse without further treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

318937</a>

3190] Two cycles of post-RPLND chemotherapy using either bleomycin, etoposide, and cisplatin (BEP) or etoposide plus cisplatin (EP) lowers the risk of relapse in men with pathological stage II disease to about 1%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

319138</a>

3192,<a href="#cit/section_6.39">

319339</a>

3194] Most patients in studies of RPLND underwent the operation at a center of excellence with a urological surgeon who had performed hundreds of such operations. The ability of less-experienced urologists to achieve similar results is unknown.</p>

3195<p id="_668" tabindex="-1">

3196In patients with pathological stage

3197I disease after RPLND, the presence of lymphatic or venous invasion or a predominance of embryonal carcinoma in the

3198primary tumor appears to predict for relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

319940</a>

3200-<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

320142</a>

3202] In a large, Testicular Cancer

3203Intergroup Study, the relapse rate among men with pathological stage I disease was 19% in those with vascular invasion

3204versus 6% in those without vascular invasion. One study reported that the relapse rate for men with pathological stage I disease was 21.2% (18 of 85 men relapsed), if their tumors were predominantly embryonal carcinoma and 29% if there was a predominance of embryonal carcinoma plus lymphovascular invasion versus 3% (5 of 141 men relapsed), if there was not a predominance of embryonal carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

320540</a>

3206,<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

320741</a>

3208] </p>

3209<p id="_677" tabindex="-1">

3210Among pathological stage II patients, the relapse rate was 32% among men with embryonal carcinoma-predominant tumors compared with 15.6% in the other stage II patients. The risk of metastatic disease (i.e., either pathological stage II disease or relapsed pathological stage I disease) in men with tumors showing a predominance of embryonal carcinoma plus lymphovascular invasion was 62% compared with 16% in men with neither risk factor. </p>

3211<p id="_670" tabindex="-1">

3212These data have shown that high-risk patients undergoing RPLND have a substantial risk of subsequently receiving chemotherapy. Data from one institution have shown that about half of men with stage I pure embryonal carcinoma undergoing RPLND will subsequently receive cisplatin-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

321343</a>

3214]</p>

3215<p id="_671" tabindex="-1">

3216Retroperitoneal dissection of

3217lymph nodes is not helpful in the management of children, and potential

3218morbidity of the surgery is not justified by the information obtained.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

321933</a>

3220] In

3221men who have undergone RPLND, chemotherapy is employed immediately on first evidence of recurrence.</p>

3222</li>

3223<li>

3224Adjuvant

3225therapy consisting of one or two courses of BEP chemotherapy in patients with clinical stage I disease.<p id="_678" tabindex="-1">

3226A randomized controlled trial compared a single cycle of BEP chemotherapy with RPLND in 382 patients. The 2-year recurrence-free survival rates were 99.5% with chemotherapy versus 91.9% with RPLND (absolute difference, 7.6%; 95% CI, 3.1%&#8211;12.1%). There were no treatment-related or cancer-specific deaths in either arm of the study.[<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

322744</a>

3228]</p>

3229<p id="_675" tabindex="-1">

3230A Swedish and Norwegian study reported results of a risk-adapted therapy protocol in which patients with nonseminomas with lymphovascular invasion underwent postorchiectomy chemotherapy with one or two cycles of BEP chemotherapy, while those without lymphovascular invasion underwent either surveillance or a single cycle of BEP.[<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

323145</a>

3232] The study included 745 patients and, with a median follow-up of 4.7 years and 2-year follow-up of 89% of patients, there were no deaths from testicular cancer, although one patient died of a stroke immediately after completing chemotherapy for relapsed disease. OS was 98.9% and cause-specific survival was 99.9%. Both of these studies were conducted at community-based hospitals and demonstrated that postorchiectomy chemotherapy could be delivered at a regional or national level without depending on centers of excellence.</p>

3233<p id="_676" tabindex="-1">

3234Several phase II studies and case series reporting the results after two cycles of BEP in intermediate- or high-risk patients have identified relapse rates ranging from 0% to 4% (average, 2.4%).[<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

323546</a>

3236] Fewer than 1% of patients in these series died of testicular cancer. While chemotherapy produces the lower relapse rate and a comparable disease-specific survival rate compared with RPLND or surveillance, it is unknown whether a brief course of chemotherapy results in late toxic effects or an increased risk of late relapse. Longer follow-up is awaited. </p>

3237</li>

3238</ol>

3239</div>

3240<p id="_659" tabindex="-1">

3241There is no consensus about the optimal management of men with stage I nonseminomas, but each of the three strategies above produces a disease-specific survival rate of about 99%. Some clinicians have advocated a risk-adapted approach such that low-risk patients undergo surveillance, while others undergo either RPLND or chemotherapy. The goal of this approach is to minimize the side effects of treatment, but risk-adapted therapy has never been demonstrated to result in better outcomes. Some experts prefer a surveillance strategy generally so as to minimize unnecessary treatment. Others prefer RPLND to obtain more accurate staging, to reduce the risk of needing chemotherapy (and, therefore, chemotherapy's side effects and toxicity) and to, theoretically, reduce the risk of late relapse. At the same time, many experts reject RPLND as insufficiently effective at lowering relapse rates and prefer chemotherapy. Surveillance and chemotherapy have been tested at the regional and national level with excellent results, however, the limited data concerning RPLND in the regional setting have shown higher than expected in-field relapse rates but no deaths.[<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

324244</a>

3243,<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

324445</a>

3245]</p>

3246<p id="_322" tabindex="-1">

3247With regard to risk stratification, data suggest that relapse rates are higher in patients with histological evidence

3248of lymphatic or venous invasion or a predominance of embryonal carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

324912</a>

3250,<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

325131</a>

3252,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

325340</a>

3254,<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

325541</a>

3256,<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

325747</a>

3258] Tumors that consist of mature teratoma appear to have a lower relapse rate.[<a href="#cit/section_6.48">

325948</a>

3260] </p>

3261</section>

3262<section id="_TrialSearch_117_sid_6">

3263<h3 id="_TrialSearch_117_sid_6_toc">

3264Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3265<p id="_TrialSearch_117_22" tabindex="-1">

3266Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3267advanced clinical trial search</a>

3268 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3269General information</a>

3270 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3271</section>

J Clin Oncol 18 (2): 358-62, 2000.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10637250&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10637250&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3433[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3434</li>

3435<li id="section_6.42">

3437[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3438</li>

3439<li id="section_6.43">

3441[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3442</li>

3443<li id="section_6.44">

3444Albers P, Siener R, Krege S, et al.: Randomized phase III trial comparing retroperitoneal lymph node dissection with one course of bleomycin and etoposide plus cisplatin chemotherapy in the adjuvant treatment of clinical stage I Nonseminomatous testicular germ cell tumors: AUO trial AH 01/94 by the German Testicular Cancer Study Group. J Clin Oncol 26 (18): 2966-72, 2008.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=18458040&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=18458040&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3470 <section id="_136">

3471

3472 <h2>

3473Treatment of Stage II Testicular Cancer</h2>

3474

3475 <div id="_section_136" class="pdq-sections">

3476<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3477<h6>

3478In This Section</h6>

3479<ul>

3480<li>

3481<a href="#_139_toc">

3482Stage II Seminoma</a>

3483</li>

3484<li>

3485<a href="#_145_toc">

3486Stage II Nonseminoma</a>

3487</li>

3488<li>

3489<a href="#_TrialSearch_136_sid_7_toc">

3490Current Clinical Trials</a>

3491</li>

3492</ul>

3493</nav>

3494<section id="_139">

3495<h3 id="_139_toc">

3496Stage II Seminoma</h3>

3497<p id="_140" tabindex="-1">

3498Stage II seminoma is divided into bulky and nonbulky disease for treatment

3499planning and expression of prognosis. Bulky disease is generally defined as

3500tumors larger than 5 cm on a computed tomography (CT) scan (i.e., stage IIC disease). Nonbulky disease can be further subdivided into stage IIA, meaning no lymph node mass larger than 2 cm, and stage IIB, meaning a lymph node mass between 2 cm and 5 cm.</p>

3501<p id="_141" tabindex="-1">

3502Nonbulky stage II disease has a cure rate of about 90% to 95% with radiation

3503therapy alone at doses of 30 Gy to 36 Gy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

35041</a>

3505-<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

35064</a>

3507] Most patients with relapsed disease can be cured with chemotherapy. Cure rates are slightly higher for patients with stage IIA disease than for those with IIB disease, but the figures are within the range given above. Risk factors for relapse include multiple enlarged nodes.

3508</p>

3509<p id="_442" tabindex="-1">

3510Results for patients with stage IIC disease have been less favorable. For example, one institution reported that among patients with stage IIC disease, 9 of 16 (56%) had a relapse following radiation therapy, compared with only 1 of 23 patients (4%) treated with chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

35113</a>

3512] A pooled analysis of earlier studies reported a 65% relapse-free survival (RFS) rate for men receiving radiation therapy for bulky stage II seminoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

35135</a>

3514] Unfortunately, only sparse contemporary data are available on the use of radiation therapy to treat bulky stage II seminomas, and there are no randomized trials comparing radiation therapy with chemotherapy in this population. Combination

3515chemotherapy with cisplatin is effective therapy in patients with bulky

3516stage II seminomas and has become the most widely accepted treatment option.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

35176</a>

3518,<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

35197</a>

3520]</p>

3521<p id="_323" tabindex="-1">

3522Residual radiological abnormalities are common at the

3523completion of chemotherapy. Many abnormalities gradually regress during a period

3524of months. Some clinicians advocate empiric attempts to resect residual masses 3 cm or larger, while others advocate close surveillance, with intervention only if the residual mass increases in size. Postchemotherapy radiation therapy is no longer favored, in part because of a retrospective

3525study of a consecutive series of 174 patients with seminoma and postchemotherapy residual

3526disease seen at ten treatment centers. The study reported that empiric radiation was not associated with

3527any medically significant improvement in progression-free survival after

3528completion of platinum-based combination chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

35294</a>

3530][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3531Level of evidence C2</a>

3532] </p>

3533<p id="_460" tabindex="-1">

3534In some series, surgical resection of specific masses has yielded a

3535significant number of patients with residual seminoma who require additional therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

35365</a>

3537]

3538Nevertheless, other reports indicate that the size of the residual mass does not

3539correlate well with active residual disease, most residual masses do not grow,

3540and frequent marker and CT scan evaluation is a viable option even when the

3541residual mass is 3 cm or larger.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

35426</a>

3543]</p>

3544<p id="_324" tabindex="-1">

3545A more recent approach has been to obtain a fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose positron emission tomography-computed tomography (18F-FDG PET-CT) scan following chemotherapy. A study of 56 patients reported that positron emission tomography (PET) scans correctly identified eight of ten patients with residual seminoma with no false positives among the 46 patients with benign masses.[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

35468</a>

3547] In this study, PET scans were 100% accurate in patients with residual masses greater than 3 cm in greatest diameter whereas residual malignant masses less than 3 cm were only detected in one of three men. This study provides support for observing men with residual 18F-FDG PET-negative masses greater than 3 cm and for performing a biopsy or resection of any 18F-FDG PET-positive mass.</p>

3548<p id="_142" tabindex="-1">

3549<strong>

3550Standard treatment options for patients with nonbulky tumors:

3551</strong>

3552</p>

3553<div class="pdq-content-list">

3554<ol id="_443">

3555<li>

3556Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by

3557radiation therapy to the retroperitoneal and ipsilateral pelvic lymph nodes. Prophylactic radiation therapy to the mediastinum is contraindicated because of cardiovascular toxic effects, and prophylactic radiation to the supraclavicular fossa is not standard. Radiation therapy to inguinal nodes is not standard unless there has been

3558some damage to the scrotum to put inguinal lymph nodes at risk.</li>

3559<li>

3560Systemic chemotherapy using three cycles of bleomycin, etoposide, and cisplatin (BEP) or four cycles of etoposide and cisplatin. This approach is generally reserved for stage IIA and IIB patients who have multiple areas of adenopathy in the retroperitoneum or a contraindication to radiation therapy such as a horseshoe or pelvic kidney, or inflammatory bowel disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

35617</a>

3562,<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

35639</a>

3564-<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

356511</a>

3566]</li>

3567<li>

3568Retroperitoneal lymph node dissection (RPLND) may be performed in those rare men who have contraindications to radiation therapy and chemotherapy.</li>

3569</ol>

3570</div>

3571<p id="_461" tabindex="-1">

3572<strong>

3573Standard treatment options for patients with bulky tumors:

3574</strong>

3575</p>

3576<div class="pdq-content-list">

3577<ol id="_444">

3578<li>

3579Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by

3580combination chemotherapy (with a cisplatin-based regimen) using three cycles of BEP or four cycles of etoposide and cisplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

35817</a>

3582,<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

35839</a>

3584-<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

358511</a>

3586]</li>

3587<li>

3588Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by radiation therapy to

3589the abdominal and pelvic lymph nodes. The recurrence rate is higher after

3590radiation therapy for men with bulky stage II tumors than radiation therapy for nonbulky tumors,

3591leading some authors to recommend primary chemotherapy for patients with bulky

3592disease (&#8805;5 cm&#8211;10 cm).[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

35933</a>

3594,<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

359512</a>

3596]</li>

3597</ol>

3598</div>

3599</section>

3600<section id="_145">

3601<h3 id="_145_toc">

3602Stage II Nonseminoma</h3>

3603<p id="_146" tabindex="-1">

3604Stage II nonseminoma is highly curable (&gt;95%). Men with stage II disease and persistently elevated serum tumor markers are generally treated as having stage III disease and receive chemotherapy. For men with normal markers after orchiectomy, nonseminomas are divided into stages IIA, IIB, and IIC for treatment purposes. In general, stage IIA patients undergo RPLND to confirm the staging. As many as 40% of clinical stage IIA patients will have benign findings at RPLND and will be restaged as having pathological stage I disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

360513</a>

3606] RPLND can thus prevent a significant number of patients with clinical stage IIA disease from receiving unnecessary chemotherapy. </p>

3607<p id="_546" tabindex="-1">

3608In contrast, patients with stage IIB and IIC nonseminoma are usually treated with systemic chemotherapy for disseminated disease because these patients have a higher relapse rate after RPLND. One study reported that by limiting RPLND to patients with earlier stage II disease and normal serum tumor markers, 5-year RFS rates increased from 78% to 100% after RPLND, while RFS did not change significantly among stage II patients receiving chemotherapy (100% vs. 98%).[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

360914</a>

3610] However, the question of whether to treat patients with stage II nonseminoma germ cell tumors with RPLND or chemotherapy has never been subjected to a randomized trial. </p>

3611<p id="_147" tabindex="-1">

3612<strong>

3613Standard treatment options:

3614</strong>

3615</p>

3616<div class="pdq-content-list">

3617<ol id="_220">

3618<li>

3619For patients with clinical stage II disease and normal postorchiectomy serum tumor markers, radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by removal of retroperitoneal lymph

3620nodes with or without fertility-preserving RPLND followed by monthly checkups,

3621which include physical examination, chest x-ray, and serum marker tests (e.g., alpha-fetoprotein, human chorionic gonadotropin, and lactate dehydrogenase). <p id="_446" tabindex="-1">

3622This

3623option of surgery and careful follow-up, reserving chemotherapy for relapse, is

3624particularly attractive for patients who have pathological stage I or IIA disease (fewer than six positive nodes at

3625RPLND, none of which are larger than 2

3626cm in diameter). Such

3627patients appear to have a relapse rate of about 10% if followed

3628without chemotherapy, and most are curable with standard chemotherapy if their disease relapses.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

362913</a>

3630,<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

363115</a>

3632] Presence of lymphatic or venous invasion and the proportion of the primary tumor that is embryonal carcinoma also help to

3633predict which patients may have disease relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

363416</a>

3635-<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

363618</a>

3637] In one study, the relapse rate in men with pathological stage I disease was 3% in men with nonembryonal carcinoma-predominant tumors, 21% in men with embryonal carcinoma-predominant tumors, and 31% in those with embryonal carcinoma-predominant tumors and lymphovascular invasion.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

363817</a>

3639,<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

364018</a>

3641] In children, surgical resection of retroperitoneal nodes is generally not

3642performed. Patients with clinical stage II disease are given chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

364319</a>

3644]</p>

3645

3646</li>

3647<li>

3648For patients with clinical and pathological stage II disease and normal postorchiectomy serum tumor markers, radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by removal of retroperitoneal lymph

3649nodes followed by two cycles of chemotherapy (i.e., etoposide and cisplatin either with or without bleomycin) and then monthly checkups. <p id="_447" tabindex="-1">

3650This option of RPLND plus adjuvant chemotherapy applies to patients who have pathologically confirmed lymph node metastases as a result of RPLND and is most attractive for patients with pathological stage IIB or IIC disease. The results of a

3651large study comparing the first treatment option with the second treatment option were published.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

365220</a>

3653] Two courses of

3654cisplatin-based chemotherapy (either cisplatin, vinblastine, bleomycin [PVB] or

3655vinblastine, dactinomycin, bleomycin, cyclophosphamide, cisplatin [VAB VI])

3656prevented a relapse in more than 95% of patients. A 49% relapse

3657rate was seen in patients assigned to observation; however, most of these patients

3658could be effectively treated, and no significant differences were found in overall survival. The study concluded that adjuvant

3659therapy will most often prevent relapse in patients treated with optimal surgery, follow-up, and

3660chemotherapy. However, observation with chemotherapy only for relapse will lead to a similar cure

3661rate.</p>

3662

3663</li>

3664<li>

3665Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by chemotherapy with subsequent surgery

3666 to remove any residual masses (if present) followed by monthly checkups.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

366713</a>

3668] <p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

3669This

3670option is useful for patients with elevated serum tumor markers and/or clinical stage IIB or IIC disease. The combination of chemotherapy plus resection of residual masses in these patients results in cure in more than 95% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

367114</a>

3672,<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

367321</a>

3674]</p>

3675<p id="_227" tabindex="-1">

3676Chemotherapy regimens include:</p>

3677<div class="pdq-content-list">

3678<ul id="_221">

3679<li>

3680BEP: Bleomycin plus etoposide plus cisplatin for three courses.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

368122</a>

3682,<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

368323</a>

3684] A modified

3685regimen has been used in children.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

368619</a>

3687]</li>

3688<li>

3689EP: Etoposide plus cisplatin for four courses in patients with a good prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

369024</a>

3691]</li>

3692</ul>

3693</div>

3694<p id="_155" tabindex="-1">

3695A randomized study has shown that bleomycin is an essential component of the

3696BEP regimen when only three courses are administered.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

369725</a>

3698]

3699</p>

3700<p id="_156" tabindex="-1">

3701Other regimens that appear to produce similar survival outcomes but are no longer considered standard include:

3702</p>

3703<div class="pdq-content-list">

3704<ul id="_157">

3705<li>

3706PVB: Cisplatin plus vinblastine plus bleomycin.

3707</li>

3708<li>

3709VAB VI: Vinblastine plus dactinomycin plus bleomycin plus cyclophosphamide plus

3710cisplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

371126</a>

3712]</li>

3713<li>

3714VPV: Vinblastine plus cisplatin plus etoposide.[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

371527</a>

3716]

3717</li>

3718</ul>

3719</div>

3720</li>

3721</ol>

3722</div>

3723<p id="_160" tabindex="-1">

3724In a randomized comparison of PVB versus BEP, equivalent anticancer

3725activity was seen but with less toxic effects with the use of BEP.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

372620</a>

3727,<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

372828</a>

3729]

3730</p>

3731<p id="_161" tabindex="-1">

3732If these patients do not achieve a complete response with chemotherapy, surgical

3733removal of residual masses should be performed. The timing of such surgery

3734requires clinical judgment but would occur most often after three or four cycles of

3735combination chemotherapy and normalization or stabilization of serum markers. The presence of persistently elevated markers is not a contraindication to resection of residual masses, but patients with rising markers at the end of chemotherapy are generally treated with salvage chemotherapy. Despite numerous studies, no sufficiently accurate predictors of the histology of residual masses have been validated. Therefore, the standard of care is to resect all residual masses

3736apparent on scans in patients who have normal or stable markers after responding to

3737chemotherapy. The presence of persistent

3738nonseminomatous germ-cell malignant elements in the resected specimen is a poor prognostic sign and is often a trigger for additional chemotherapy. However, men with only microscopic residual cancer have a much more favorable prognosis than men with more substantial residual disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

373929</a>

3740,<a href="#cit/section_7.30">

374130</a>

3742] Identifying the patients who benefit from additional chemotherapy is not possible from existing data.

3743</p>

3744<p id="_325" tabindex="-1">

3745In some cases, chemotherapy is

3746initiated before orchiectomy because of life-threatening metastatic disease.

3747When this is done, orchiectomy after initiation or completion of

3748chemotherapy is advisable to remove the primary tumor. There is a

3749higher incidence (approximately 50%) of residual cancer in the testicle than in

3750remaining radiographically detectable retroperitoneal masses after platinum-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

375131</a>

3752]</p>

3753</section>

3754<section id="_TrialSearch_136_sid_7">

3755<h3 id="_TrialSearch_136_sid_7_toc">

3756Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3757<p id="_TrialSearch_136_22" tabindex="-1">

3758Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3759advanced clinical trial search</a>

3760 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3761General information</a>

3762 about clinical trials is also available.</p>
