3898 <section id="_628">

3899

3900 <h2>

3901Treatment of Stage III Testicular Cancer</h2>

3902

3903 <div id="_section_628" class="pdq-sections">

3904<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3905<h6>

3906In This Section</h6>

3907<ul>

3908<li>

3909<a href="#_618_toc">

3910Clinical Trials of Chemotherapy for Disseminated Testis and Extragonadal Germ Cell Tumors</a>

3911</li>

3912<li>

3913<a href="#_622_toc">

3914Special Considerations During Chemotherapy</a>

3915</li>

3916<li>

3917<a href="#_468_toc">

3918Residual Masses After Chemotherapy in Men With Seminomas</a>

3919</li>

3920<li>

3921<a href="#_350_toc">

3922Residual Masses After Chemotherapy in Men With Nonseminomas</a>

3923</li>

3924<li>

3925<a href="#_TrialSearch_628_sid_8_toc">

3926Current Clinical Trials</a>

3927</li>

3928</ul>

3929</nav>

3930<section id="_630">

3931<p id="_631" tabindex="-1">

3932Stage III seminoma and nonseminomas are usually curable but have different criteria for estimating prognosis. </p>

3933<p id="_632" tabindex="-1">

3934Patients with disseminated seminomas can be divided into good-risk and intermediate-risk groups based on whether nonpulmonary visceral metastases are present. Good-risk patients (i.e., those with metastases only to lymph nodes and/or lungs) have 5-year progression-free survival (PFS) and overall survival (OS) rates of 82% and 86%, respectively. Intermediate-risk seminoma patients have 5-year PFS and OS rates of 67% and 72%, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

39351</a>

3936]</p>

3937<p id="_633" tabindex="-1">

3938Patients with disseminated nonseminomas can be divided into good-, intermediate-, and poor-risk groups based on whether nonpulmonary visceral metastases are present, the site of the primary tumor (i.e., mediastinal vs. either gonadal or retroperitoneal), and the level of serum tumor markers.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

39391</a>

3940]</p>

3941<div class="pdq-content-list">

3942<ul id="_634">

3943<li>

3944Poor-risk: Men with mediastinal primary tumors, nonpulmonary visceral metastases, or very highly elevated serum tumor markers are considered to be at poor risk. For more information, see the <a href="/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq#_29">

3945Stage Information for Testicular Cancer</a>

3946 section.</li>

3947<li>

3948Intermediate-risk: Men with intermediate tumor markers levels are considered to be at intermediate risk.</li>

3949<li>

3950Good-risk: Men with good-risk disease have a testis or retroperitoneal primary tumor, metastases limited to lymph nodes and/or lungs, and tumor markers that are in the good-risk range.</li>

3951</ul>

3952</div>

3953<p id="_635" tabindex="-1">

3954In the 1997 analysis that established these risk groups, the 5-year OS rates were 92%, 80% and 48% in the good-, intermediate-, and poor-risk groups, respectively. The PFS rates were 89%, 75% and 41% in the good-, intermediate-, and poor-risk groups, respectively. However, a 2006 pooled analysis of chemotherapy trials reported improved outcomes compared with the 1997 paper: survival rates in the good-, intermediate-, and poor-risk groups were 94%, 83%, and 71%, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

39552</a>

3956]</p>

3957</section>

3958<section id="_618">

3959<h3 id="_618_toc">

3960Clinical Trials of Chemotherapy for Disseminated Testis and Extragonadal Germ Cell Tumors</h3>

3961<p id="_619" tabindex="-1">

3962Four cycles of bleomycin, etoposide, and cisplatin (BEP) chemotherapy as a standard-of-care treatment option for patients with metastatic testicular germ cell tumors was established by a randomized trial showing that it produced similar outcomes with fewer toxic effects in comparison with cisplatin, vinblastine, and bleomycin (PVB).[<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

39633</a>

3964] Two randomized trials comparing four courses of BEP with four courses of etoposide plus ifosfamide plus cisplatin (VIP) showed

3965similar OS and time-to-treatment failure for the two regimens in

3966patients with intermediate- and poor-risk advanced disseminated germ cell tumors who had not received prior

3967chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

39684</a>

3969-<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

39706</a>

3971][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3972Level of evidence A1</a>

3973] Hematologic toxic effects were

3974substantially worse with the VIP regimen.

3975For good-risk patients, two randomized trials compared three versus four cycles of BEP and reported no significant benefit from longer treatment in that population.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

39767</a>

3977-<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

39789</a>

3979]</p>

3980<p id="_620" tabindex="-1">

3981Numerous attempts have been made to develop a regimen superior to BEP for men with poor-prognosis germ cell tumors but none have been successful. Most recently, four cycles of BEP was compared with two cycles of BEP followed by two cycles of high-dose cyclophosphamide, etoposide, and carboplatin, but there was no difference in survival between the two arms.[<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

398210</a>

3983] Earlier trials of higher dose cisplatin or long-term maintenance chemotherapy were similarly disappointing.</p>

3984<p id="_621" tabindex="-1">

3985For patients with good-risk disease, the goal of clinical trials has been to minimize the toxic effects of treatment without sacrificing the therapeutic effectiveness. As noted above, no difference in outcome was seen when comparing three versus four cycles of BEP chemotherapy. However, attempts to eliminate bleomycin produced more ambiguous and usually disappointing results. A randomized controlled trial comparing three cycles of BEP with three cycles of etoposide and cisplatin (EP) reported lower OS rates (95% vs. 86%, <em>

3986P</em>

3987 = .01) in the EP arm.[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

398811</a>

3989] Similarly, when three cycles of BEP was compared with four cycles of EP in a randomized trial in more than 260 patients, there were 6 relapses and 5 deaths in the bleomycin arm compared with 14 relapses and 12 deaths in the EP arm, but these differences were not statistically significant.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

399012</a>

3991] Several other studies have compared bleomycin-containing regimens to etoposide and cisplatin and in every trial, the trend in survival has favored the bleomycin arm, but the differences have not usually been statistically significant.[<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

399213</a>

3993-<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

399415</a>

3995] These results have led to some controversy as to whether three cycles of BEP is superior to four cycles of EP. </p>

3996</section>

3997<section id="_622">

3998<h3 id="_622_toc">

3999Special Considerations During Chemotherapy</h3>

4000<p id="_623" tabindex="-1">

4001In most patients, an orchiectomy is performed before starting chemotherapy. If the diagnosis has been made by biopsy of a metastatic site (or on the basis of highly elevated serum tumor markers and radiological imaging consistent with an advanced-stage germ cell tumor) and chemotherapy has been initiated, subsequent orchiectomy is generally performed because chemotherapy may not eradicate the primary tumor. Case reports illustrate that viable tumor has been found on postchemotherapy orchiectomy despite complete response of metastatic lesions.[<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

400216</a>

4003]</p>

4004<p id="_624" tabindex="-1">

4005Some retrospective data suggest that the experience of the treating institution

4006may impact the outcome of patients with stage III nonseminoma. Data from 380 patients

4007treated from 1990 to 1994 on the same study protocol at 49 institutions in the

4008European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer and the Medical

4009Research Council were analyzed.[<a href="#cit/section_8.17">

401017</a>

4011] Overall, the 2-year survival rate for the 55

4012patients treated at institutions that entered fewer than five patients onto the

4013protocol was 62% (95% confidence interval [CI], 48%&#8211;75%) versus 77% (95% CI, 72%&#8211;81%) in the

4014institutions that entered five or more patients onto the protocol. </p>

4015<p id="_625" tabindex="-1">

4016Similarly, a population-based study of testis cancer in Japan in the 1990s reported a significant association between survival and the number of testis cancer patients treated. The relative 5-year survival rate was 98.8% at high-volume hospitals compared with 79.7% at low-volume hospitals. After adjusting for stage and age, the hazard ratio for death in a high-volume hospital was 0.11 (95% CI, 0.025&#8211;0.495).[<a href="#cit/section_8.18">

401718</a>

4018] Several other studies have reported similar findings.[<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

401919</a>

4020-<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

402121</a>

4022] As in any

4023nonrandomized study design, patient selection factors and factors leading

4024patients to choose treatment at one center versus another can make interpretation

4025of these results difficult.

4026</p>

4027<p id="_626" tabindex="-1">

4028Many patients with poor-risk, nonseminomatous testicular germ cell tumors who

4029have a serum beta-human chorionic gonadotropin (beta-hCG) level higher than 50,000

4030IU/mL at the initiation of cisplatin-based therapy

4031(BEP or PVB) will still have an elevated beta-hCG level at the completion of

4032therapy, showing an initial rapid decrease in beta-hCG followed by a plateau.[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

403322</a>

4034]

4035In the absence of other signs of progressing disease, monthly evaluation with

4036initiation of salvage therapy, if and when there is serologic progression, may be

4037appropriate. Many patients, however, will remain disease free without further

4038therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

403922</a>

4040][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4041Level of evidence C3</a>

4042]

4043</p>

4044</section>

4045<section id="_468">

4046<h3 id="_468_toc">

4047Residual Masses After Chemotherapy in Men With Seminomas</h3>

4048<p id="_469" tabindex="-1">

4049Residual radiological abnormalities are common at the completion of chemotherapy.

4050Such masses are not treated unless they grow or are histopathologically shown to contain viable cancer. In a combined retrospective consecutive series of 174 seminoma

4051patients with postchemotherapy residual disease seen at ten treatment centers,

4052empiric radiation was not associated with any medically significant improvement

4053in PFS after completion of platinum-based combination

4054chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.23">

405523</a>

4056][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4057Level of evidence C2</a>

4058] In some series, surgical

4059resection of specific masses has yielded a significant number of patients with residual

4060seminoma that require additional therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

406124</a>

4062] Larger masses are more likely to harbor viable cancer, but there is no size criteria with high sensitivity and specificity. Fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose-positron emission tomography (18F-FDG PET) scans have been shown to be helpful in identifying patients who harbor viable cancers, but the false-positive rate is substantial in some series.[<a href="#cit/section_8.25">

406325</a>

4064-<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

406527</a>

4066] The strength of positron emission tomography (PET) scans in residual seminoma masses is that they have a very high sensitivity and a low false-negative rate. Thus, for men with residual masses for whom resection is being planned, a negative PET scan provides evidence that surgery is not necessary. </p>

4067<p id="_470" tabindex="-1">

4068Although larger residual masses are more likely to harbor viable seminoma, the size of the residual mass is of limited prognostic value.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

406924</a>

4070-<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

407126</a>

4072] Most residual masses do not grow, and regular marker and computed tomography (CT) scan evaluation is a viable

4073management option for large or small masses.[<a href="#cit/section_8.28">

407428</a>

4075]

4076An alternative approach is to operate on larger masses, to resect them when possible, and to perform biopsies of unresectable masses. Postchemotherapy masses are often difficult or impossible to resect because of a dense desmoplastic reaction. Historically, such surgery has been characterized by a high rate of complications or additional procedures such as nephrectomy or arterial or venous grafting.[<a href="#cit/section_8.29">

407729</a>

4078]</p>

4079</section>

4080<section id="_350">

4081<h3 id="_350_toc">

4082Residual Masses After Chemotherapy in Men With Nonseminomas</h3>

4083<p id="_582" tabindex="-1">

4084Residual masses following chemotherapy in men with nonseminomatous germ cell tumors often contain viable cancer or teratoma, and the standard of care is to resect all such masses when possible. However, there are no randomized controlled trials evaluating this issue. Instead, the practice is based on the fact that viable neoplasm is often found at surgery in these patients, and the presumption is that such tumors would progress if not resected. If serum tumor markers are rising, salvage chemotherapy is usually given, but stable or slowly declining tumor markers are not a contraindication to resection of residual masses.</p>

4085<p id="_583" tabindex="-1">

4086Case series of men undergoing postchemotherapy resections have reported that roughly 10% will have viable germ cell cancer, 45% will have teratomas, and 45% will have no viable tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_8.30">

408730</a>

4088] Numerous attempts have been made to identify the patients who need surgery and the patients who can be safely observed. Variables predictive of finding only necrosis or fibrosis at surgery include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_8.31">

408931</a>

4090]</p>

4091<div class="pdq-content-list">

4092<ul id="_584">

4093<li>

4094Absence of any teratoma in the primary tumor.</li>

4095<li>

4096Normal prechemotherapy serum alpha-fetoprotein, beta-hCG, and lactase dehydrogenase.</li>

4097<li>

4098A small, residual mass.</li>

4099<li>

4100A large diminishment in mass size during chemotherapy.</li>

4101</ul>

4102</div>

4103<p id="_585" tabindex="-1">

4104However, only a small proportion of men have favorable enough features to have less than a 10% chance of having viable neoplasm in their residual masses, and thus the utility of current models has been questioned.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

410524</a>

4106,<a href="#cit/section_8.32">

410732</a>

4108]</p>

4109<p id="_586" tabindex="-1">

4110When multiple sites of residual disease are present, all residual masses are generally resected. If it is not surgically feasible, resection is generally not performed. Some patients may have discordant pathological findings (e.g., fibrosis/necrosis,

4111teratoma, or carcinoma) in residual masses in the abdomen versus the

4112chest. Some medical centers perform simultaneous retroperitoneal

4113and thoracic operations to remove residual masses,[<a href="#cit/section_8.28">

411428</a>

4115,<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

411633</a>

4117] but most do not. Although

4118the agreement among the histologies of residual masses found after chemotherapy

4119above the diaphragm versus those found below the diaphragm is only moderate (kappa statistic, 0.42),

4120 some evidence exists that if retroperitoneal resection is performed first,

4121results can be used to guide decisions about whether to perform a

4122thoracotomy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

412334</a>

4124]</p>

4125<p id="_587" tabindex="-1">

4126In a multi-institutional case series of surgery to remove

4127postchemotherapy residual masses in 159 patients, necrosis only was found at

4128thoracotomy in about 90% of patients who had necrosis only in their

4129retroperitoneal masses. The figure was about 95% if the original testicular

4130primary tumor had contained no teratomatous elements. Conversely, the

4131histology of residual masses at thoracotomy did not predict nearly as well the histology of retroperitoneal masses.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

413234</a>

4133] Nonetheless, some centers continue to support resection of all residual masses, even if necrosis is found in the retroperitoneum.[<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

413435</a>

4135]</p>

4136<p id="_588" tabindex="-1">

4137The presence of persistent malignant

4138elements in the resected specimen is considered by some clinicians to be an indication for additional

4139chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.36">

414036</a>

4141] However, there are no prospective trials investigating the benefit of such treatment. In some cases, chemotherapy is initiated before the

4142orchiectomy because of life-threatening metastatic disease. When this is done,

4143orchiectomy after initiation or completion of chemotherapy is advisable to remove the primary tumor. A

4144physiological blood-testis barrier seems to appear, and there is a higher incidence (approximately

414550%) of residual cancer in the testicle than in remaining radiographically

4146detectable retroperitoneal masses after platinum-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

414716</a>

4148] Some

4149investigators have suggested that in children, 90% of whom have yolk sac

4150tumors, radiation therapy should be given to residual masses after chemotherapy

4151rather than surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_8.37">

415237</a>

4153]</p>

4154</section>

4155<section id="_679">

4156<p id="_680" tabindex="-1">

4157<strong>

4158Standard treatment options for initial treatment for nonseminoma patients with good-risk disease:</strong>

4159</p>

4160<div class="pdq-content-list">

4161<ul id="_681">

4162<li>

4163Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by multidrug chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

416438</a>

4165]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4166<p class="pdq-list-title">

4167Chemotherapy combinations include:</p>

4168<ul id="_682">

4169<li>

4170BEP: Bleomycin plus etoposide plus cisplatin for three 21-day cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

41717</a>

4172-<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

41739</a>

4174,<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

417511</a>

4176]</li>

4177<li>

4178 EP: Etoposide plus cisplatin for four 21-day cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

417913</a>

4180,<a href="#cit/section_8.39">

418139</a>

4182,<a href="#cit/section_8.40">

418340</a>

4184] Four cycles of EP should be considered for men with good-risk metastatic seminoma who have a contraindication to receiving bleomycin.</li>

4185</ul>

4186</div>

4187</li>

4188</ul>

4189</div>

4190<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

4191<strong>

4192Standard treatment options for initial treatment for nonseminoma patients with intermediate- and poor-risk disease:</strong>

4193</p>

4194<div class="pdq-content-list">

4195<ul id="_684">

4196<li>

4197Radical inguinal orchiectomy followed by multidrug chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

419838</a>

4199]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4200<p class="pdq-list-title">

4201Chemotherapy combinations include:</p>

4202<ul id="_685">

4203<li>

4204BEP: Bleomycin plus etoposide plus cisplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

42053</a>

4206,<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

42074</a>

4208,<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

420941</a>

4210,<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

421142</a>

4212]</li>

4213<li>

4214VIP: Etoposide plus ifosfamide plus cisplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

42155</a>

4216,<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

421741</a>

4218] Four cycles of VIP should be considered for patients with intermediate-risk metastatic seminoma who have a contraindication to receiving bleomycin. </li>

4219</ul>

4220</div>

4221</li>

4222</ul>

4223</div>

4224<p id="_686" tabindex="-1">

4225<strong>

4226Management of residual masses following chemotherapy for patients with seminoma</strong>

4227</p>

4228<div class="pdq-content-list">

4229<ul id="_687">

4230<li>

4231In

4232patients with seminoma, the residual masses after chemotherapy are usually fibrotic

4233but may contain

4234residual seminoma that requires additional therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.43">

423543</a>

4236,<a href="#cit/section_8.44">

423744</a>

4238] There are three standard management strategies:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4239<ul id="_688" class="list-dash">

4240<li>

4241Observation with no additional treatment or biopsies unless the residual mass(es) increase(s) in size.</li>

4242<li>

4243Observation of masses smaller than 3 cm and surgical resection of masses larger than 3 cm.</li>

4244<li>

4245 18F-FDG PET scan 2 months after chemotherapy is completed with observation of PET-negative masses and resection of PET-positive masses.</li>

4246</ul>

4247</div>

4248</li>

4249</ul>

4250</div>

4251</section>

4252<p id="_338" tabindex="-1">

4253<strong>

4254Management of residual masses following chemotherapy for patients with nonseminoma</strong>

4255</p>

4256<div class="pdq-content-list">

4257<ul id="_339">

4258<li>

4259Patients with residual masses following chemotherapy should have all such masses resected if technically feasible. If some, but not all, residual masses can be resected, surgery is not usually performed. The rationale for surgery in this setting is that about half of the masses will contain viable tumor, either teratoma or cancer. </li>

4260</ul>

4261</div>

4268 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4457

4458 <section id="_206">

4459

4460 <h2>

4461Treatment of Recurrent Testicular Cancer</h2>

4462

4463 <div id="_section_206" class="pdq-sections">

4464<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4465<h6>

4466In This Section</h6>

4467<ul>

4468<li>

4469<a href="#_TrialSearch_206_sid_9_toc">

4470Current Clinical Trials</a>

4471</li>

4472</ul>

4473</nav>

4474<p id="_208" tabindex="-1">

4475Deciding on further treatment depends on many factors, including the specific

4476cancer, previous treatment, site of recurrence, and individual patient

4477considerations. Salvage regimens consisting of ifosfamide, cisplatin, and

4478either etoposide or vinblastine can induce long-term complete responses in

4479about 25% of patients with disease that has persisted or recurred

4480following other cisplatin-based regimens. Patients who have had an initial

4481complete response to first-line chemotherapy and those without extensive

4482disease have the most favorable outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

44831</a>

4484,<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

44852</a>

4486] This regimen is now the standard

4487initial salvage regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

44882</a>

4489,<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

44903</a>

4491] Few, if any, patients with recurrent

4492nonseminomatous germ cell tumors of extragonadal origin, however, achieve long-term

4493disease-free survival (DFS) using vinblastine, ifosfamide, and cisplatin if their

4494disease recurred after they received an initial regimen containing etoposide

4495and cisplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

44962</a>

4497][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4498Level of evidence C2</a>

4499]

4500</p>

4501<p id="_689" tabindex="-1">

4502High-dose chemotherapy with

4503autologous marrow transplantation has also been used in uncontrolled case series in the

4504setting of recurrent disease.[<a href="#cit/section_9.4">

45054</a>

4506-<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

450711</a>

4508] However, a randomized controlled trial comparing conventional doses of salvage chemotherapy with high-dose chemotherapy with autologous marrow rescue showed more toxic effects and treatment-related deaths in the high-dose arm without any improvement in response rate or overall survival.[<a href="#cit/section_9.12">

450912</a>

4510][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4511Level of evidence A1</a>

4512] In some highly selected patients with chemorefractory

4513disease confined to a single site, surgical resection may yield long-term

4514DFS.[<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

451513</a>

4516,<a href="#cit/section_9.14">

451714</a>

4518] One case series suggests that a maintenance regimen of daily oral

4519etoposide (taken 21 days out of 28 days) may benefit patients who achieve a complete

4520remission after salvage therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_9.15">

452115</a>

4522]</p>

4523<p id="_209" tabindex="-1">

4524A special case of late relapse may include patients who relapse more than 2 years

4525after achieving complete remission; this population represents less than 5% of

4526patients who are in complete remission after 2 years. Results with

4527chemotherapy are poor in this patient subset, and surgical treatment appears to

4528be superior, if technically feasible.[<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

452916</a>

4530] Teratoma may be

4531amenable to surgery at relapse, and teratoma also has a better prognosis than carcinoma after late

4532relapse. Teratoma is a relatively resistant histological subtype,

4533so chemotherapy may not be appropriate.

4534</p>

4535<p id="_210" tabindex="-1">

4536Clinical trials are appropriate and should be considered whenever possible,

4537including phase I and phase II studies for those patients who do not achieve a complete

4538remission with induction therapy, or for those who do not achieve a complete remission

4539following etoposide and cisplatin for their initial relapse, or for patients who

4540have a second relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_9.17">

454117</a>

4542] </p>

4543<section id="_TrialSearch_206_sid_9">

4544<h3 id="_TrialSearch_206_sid_9_toc">

4545Current Clinical Trials</h3>

4546<p id="_TrialSearch_206_22" tabindex="-1">

4547Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4548advanced clinical trial search</a>

4549 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4550General information</a>

4551 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4552</section>

4631 <section id="_237">

4632

4633 <h2>

4634Changes to This Summary (03/09/2023)</h2>

4635

4636 <div id="_section_237" class="pdq-sections">

4637<p id="_238" tabindex="-1">

4638The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as

4639new information becomes available. This section describes the latest

4640changes made to this summary as of the date above.

4641</p>

4642<p id="_2015" tabindex="-1">

4643<strong>

4644<a href="/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq#_1">

4645General Information About Testicular Cancer</a>

4646</strong>

4647</p>

4648<p id="_2018" tabindex="-1">

4649Updated <a href="/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq#_450">

4650statistics</a>

4651 with estimated new cases and deaths for 2023 (cited American Cancer Society as reference 1).</p>

4652<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

4653This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

4654PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

4655, which is

4656editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

4657the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

4658information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

4659maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

4660About This PDQ Summary</a>

4661 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

4662PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

4663 pages.

4664</p>

4665</div>

4666

4667 </section>

4669

4670

4671 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

4672

4673 <h2>

4674About This PDQ Summary</h2>

4675

4676 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

4677<section id="_AboutThis_2">

4678<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

4679Purpose of This Summary</h3>

4680<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

4681This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of testicular cancer. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

4682</section>

4683<section id="_AboutThis_4">

4684<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

4685Reviewers and Updates</h3>

4686<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

4687This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

4688PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

4689, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

4690<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

4691 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

4692<div class="pdq-content-list">

4693<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

4694<li>

4695be discussed at a meeting,</li>

4696<li>

4697be cited with text, or</li>

4698<li>

4699replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

4700</ul>

4701</div>

4702<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

4703Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

4704<p id="" tabindex="-1">

4705The lead reviewers for Testicular Cancer Treatment are:</p>

4706<div class="pdq-content-list">

4707<ul id="">

4708<li>

4709Yasser Ged, MD</li>

4710<li>

4711Timothy Gilligan, MD (Cleveland Clinic Taussig Cancer Institute)</li>

4712</ul>

4713</div>

4714<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

4715Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

4716Email Us</a>

4717. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

4718</section>

4719<section id="_AboutThis_10">

4720<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

4721Levels of Evidence</h3>

4722<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

4723Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

4724formal evidence ranking system</a>

4725 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

4726</section>

4727<section id="_AboutThis_12">

4728<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

4729Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

4730<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

4731PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

4732<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

4733The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

4734<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

4735PDQ&#174; Adult Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Testicular Cancer Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq">

4736https://www.cancer.gov/types/testicular/hp/testicular-treatment-pdq</a>

4737. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389220]</p>

4738<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

4739Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

4740Visuals Online</a>

4741, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

4742

4743</p>

4744</section>

4745<section id="_AboutThis_17">

4746<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

4747Disclaimer</h3>

4748<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

4749Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

4750Managing Cancer Care</a>

4751 page.</p>

4752</section>
